import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 1D convolution operation with square input and asymmetric kernel, optionally with dilation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 1D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, length).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, length_out).
        """
        return self.conv1d_transpose(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 32
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = 5
length = 131072
stride = 1
padding = 0
dilation = 3

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, length)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

For stride=1, transposed convolution is equivalent to a regular convolution with the weight flipped appropriately, but actually it maps more directly to a dense matrix multiplication where each input element contributes to a patch of output elements. One option is to use the fact that transposed conv with stride=1 is mathematically equivalent to a standard conv with flipped kernel and different padding. Let me think about it differently. If I want output[o] = sum_k w[k] * input[o - d*k] (my transposed conv formula), then for a standard conv: out[o] = sum_k w'[k] * in[o + d*k - P] Set in = input, out = output. We need: sum_k w[k] * input[o - d*k] = sum_k w'[k] * input[o + d*k - P] Let j = K-1-k. Then: sum_k w[k] * input[o - d*k] = sum_j w[K-1-j] * input[o - d*(K-1-j)] = sum_j w[K-1-j] * input[o + d*j - d*(K-1)] So if we set P = d*(K-1) = 12 and w'[j] = w[K-1-j] (flipped weights), then: Conv1d with padding=12, dilation=d, and flipped weights gives the same result as ConvTranspose1d with padding=0, dilation=d, and original weights! Let me verify with a small example. K=2, d=1. Transpose conv: out[0] = w[0]*in[0] out[1] = w[1]*in[0] + w[0]*in[1] out[2] = w[1]*in[1] + w[0]*in[2] ... This matches! (Assuming zero padding for out-of-bounds inputs in conv). So I can rewrite this as a standard Conv1d with: - padding = dilation * (kernel_size - 1) = 12 - weights flipped along kernel dimension - stride = 1 - dilation = 3 And PyTorch Conv1d is already highly optimized. For a standard conv1d kernel with stride=1, padding=12, dilation=3: output[n, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} sum_k w[c_out, c_in, k] * input[n, c_in, o + d*k - P] If I flip the weights from the transposed conv weights, I get the same result. A common approach for convolutions is to use im2col + GEMM. But with kernel_size=5 and dilation=3, the effective kernel size is 13. For 1D conv, im2col would expand each input position into a column of size 5 (the actual kernel size, not effective). Then GEMM of (out_channels, in_channels*5) x (in_channels*5, output_length) gives the result. Wait, for the transposed conv with the direct formula: output[o] = sum_{c_in} sum_k input[c_in, o - d*k] * w[c_in, c_out, k] We can think of this as: For each c_out, we compute output[c_out, :] = sum_{c_in} sum_k w[c_in, c_out, k] * input[c_in, :] shifted by d*k. With im2col/GEMM: - Unfold the dilated input into a matrix. For each output position o, we need input[:, o], input[:, o-d], input[:, o-2d], ..., input[:, o-(K-1)d]. But these are not contiguous. Actually, for GEMM, we can create a matrix A of shape (out_channels, in_channels*K) where A[c_out, c_in*K + k] = w[c_in, c_out, k]. And a matrix B of shape (in_channels*K, output_length) where B[c_in*K + k, o] = input[c_in, o - d*k]. Then output = A @ B. This is exactly what im2col does. But constructing B requires scattering the input elements, which is not free. Alternatively, we can use GEMM where we read the input directly without forming B explicitly. This is what CUTLASS and cuDNN do. For this specific problem, given the small kernel size (5) and moderate channel counts (32, 64), a direct CUDA kernel might be competitive. Let me think about how to structure the kernel. One approach: For each output element, spawn one thread. Each thread computes: out[o] = sum_{c_in} sum_k in[c_in, o - d*k] * w[c_in, c_out, k] Since kernel_size=5 is small, each thread loads 5 input elements per channel and performs 5 MACs. With c_in=32, each thread loads 160 input elements and does 160 MACs. But the input accesses are scattered because of dilation. For o and o+1, the accessed input indices differ by 1, 1, 1, 1, 1 (all shifted by 1). Wait, let me recalculate: - Thread for o accesses: o, o-3, o-6, o-9, o-12 - Thread for o+1 accesses: o+1, o-2, o-5, o-8, o-11 These are completely different memory locations (shifted by 1). But threads within a warp access consecutive o values, so they access consecutive input locations, just at different offsets: - Thread 0 (o): in[o], in[o-3], in[o-6], in[o-9], in[o-12] - Thread 1 (o+1): in[o+1], in[o-2], in[o-5], in[o-8], in[o-11] - Thread 2 (o+2): in[o+2], in[o-1], in[o-4], in[o-7], in[o-10] - Thread 3 (o+3): in[o+3], in[o], in[o-3], in[o-6], in[o-9] - Thread 4 (o+4): in[o+4], in[o+1], in[o-2], in[o-5], in[o-8] For memory access, within a warp: - Access 1: in[o], in[o+1], in[o+2], in[o+3], in[o+4] → coalesced - Access 2: in[o-3], in[o-2], in[o-1], in[o], in[o+1] → coalesced - etc. So within a warp, memory accesses are coalesced! This is important. The threads in a warp access consecutive memory locations for each of the 5 input reads (with appropriate alignment). This means the kernel can efficiently stream memory. Per thread, for each c_in: - Read 5 floats: 20 bytes - Compute 5 MACs - Total per c_in: 20 bytes read, 20 FLOPs For all 32 c_in channels: 640 bytes read, 640 FLOPs. Output is 4 bytes per thread. But output is written, not read. Weight is 32*64*5*4 = ~41KB. This can fit in L2 cache or even L1. The memory bandwidth required per output element: each output element needs ~640 bytes of input reads (but input is reused across output channels). For 64 output channels, each input element is read 64 times. Wait, let's think about this from the perspective of the entire kernel: - Total input size: 32 * 32 * 131072 * 4 = 536.9 MB - Total output size: 32 * 64 * 131084 * 4 = 1.074 GB - Total weight size: 32 * 64 * 5 * 4 = 40.96 KB If we process the entire thing naively (one thread per output element), each input element is read multiple times: - For each (n, c_in) and each valid kernel position, input[n, c_in, i] contributes to 5 output positions (o = i, i+3, i+6, i+9, i+12) for EACH c_out. - So total input reads: 32 (batch) * 32 (c_in) * 131072 (length) * 5 (kernel) * 64 (c_out) * 4 bytes - But this is wrong because each thread reads independently. Actually, from a throughput perspective: - Each warp of 32 threads processes 32 output elements. - For each c_in and each k, they read 32 consecutive input elements (coalesced). - Total reads per warp per c_in: 32 * 5 floats = 640 floats = 2.56 KB. - Total MACs per warp per c_in: 32 * 5 = 160 MACs. - For 32 c_in channels: 32 * 2.56 KB = 81.92 KB read, 5120 MACs. - For 64 c_out channels: each warp writes 128 bytes. The compute intensity (FLOPs per byte) is: For each warp processing a chunk: - Input reads: 32 c_in * 5 reads * 32 threads * 4 bytes = 20,480 bytes - Weight reads: 32 c_in * 5 * 64 c_out * 4 bytes = 163,840 bytes. But weights are reused heavily across warps and can stay in cache. - Output writes: 32 threads * 64 c_out * 4 bytes = 8,192 bytes If weights are in L1/L2 cache, the main memory traffic is input + output. Per warp per c_in channel block: ~20KB input + 8KB output = 28KB memory traffic for 5120 MACs from c_in=32. 5120 MACs = 10240 FLOPs. 10240 / 28672 = 0.36 FLOP/byte. This is very memory bound. But wait, the weights also need to be read at some point. If 64*32*5 weights = 40KB are read once from memory and stay in L2, that's negligible. So this kernel is bandwidth bound. The key is to maximize memory bandwidth utilization by: 1. Coalesced memory access 2. Minimizing overhead 3. Maximizing occupancy Actually, I miscalculated. For each output element, we do in_channels * kernel_size = 32 * 5 = 160 MACs. Each MAC requires reading one float from input. But input is reused across output channels! If we compute multiple output channels in the same thread block, we can reuse the loaded input data. Let me think about a tiled approach: For a thread block, process a tile of output positions (e.g., 64 or 128) and a subset of output channels (e.g., 8 or 16). Tile dimensions: - Output positions: T_out (e.g., 64, 128, 256) - Output channels: T_cout (e.g., 8, 16) Within a thread block: 1. Load a tile of input data for all c_in channels. 2. Load a tile of weights for the current c_in channels and current c_out channels. 3. Compute partial sums. 4. Accumulate over c_in channels. For T_out=128 and T_cout=16 with 32 c_in: - Input tile size: 32 c_in * (128 + 12) floats ≈ 32 * 140 * 4 = 17.9 KB - Weight tile size: 32 c_in * 16 c_out * 5 floats = 10.2 KB - Shared memory total: ~28KB, well within 228KB. Wait, but input with dilation is tricky. For output positions o=0..127, the maximum input index accessed is 127. For k=0..4 with d=3, inputs needed are from 0 to 127 (since o-d*k <= 127-0 = 127). But due to dilation, the relevant inputs are o, o-3, o-6, o-9, o-12. For o in [0, 127], the required input indices range from -12 to 127. Valid ones are from 0 to 127. Wait, o=0 requires input[0]. o=1 requires input[1]. o=2 requires input[2]. o=3 requires input[3], input[0]. So for T_out = {0, ..., 127}, the maximum input index is 127 (for k=0) and minimum valid is 0. Hmm, but the issue is that for a given o, we need 5 specific input indices, and these are not contiguous. If I load input[0..127] into shared memory, then for each o, I need to access: shared_mem[o], shared_mem[o-3], shared_mem[o-6], shared_mem[o-9], shared_mem[o-12] Since shared memory is fast, this works! And we avoid redundant global memory loads. But actually, for consecutive thread blocks, there's overlap. Block 0 reads input[0..127]. Block 1 reads input[1..128] or something. For 32 c_in channels: 32 * 140 * 4 = 17,920 bytes. For weights: if we process T_cout=16 output channels with all 32 c_in and K=5: 32 * 16 * 5 * 4 = 10,240 bytes. Total shared memory: 28,160 bytes. With 228KB shared memory, we can even use larger tiles or multiple c_in batches. Actually, a key optimization: since the kernel_size is only 5, and we're doing a transposed conv, we might want to compute by iterating over output channels in shared groups. Let me think about this more carefully with GEMM perspective. For a direct kernel, here's my plan: **Kernel Design: Tiled approach** - Each thread block processes: - Batch elements: 1 (or more, but 1 is simpler) - Output channels: a tile (e.g., 8 or 16) - Output positions: a tile (e.g., 64, 128, 256) - Threads in a block arranged as: - ThreadIdx.x: output positions within tile - ThreadIdx.y: output channels within tile - Algorithm: 1. For each c_in channel (or a group of c_in channels): a. Load input slice for this c_in into shared memory (range needed for the output tile) b. Load weights for (c_in, c_out_tile) into shared memory c. Each thread computes partial dot products The shared memory approach helps because input is reused across all output channels in the tile. But there might be an even simpler approach: use CUTLASS or cuBLAS GEMM with im2col. But for kernel_size=5, the overhead of im2col might be significant. Let me think about the im2col approach: 1. For each output position o, create a column of size in_channels * K = 32 * 5 = 160. 2. The column contains input[c_in, o - d*k] for all c_in and k. 3. This gives a matrix of size (160, 131084). 4. Multiply by weight matrix of size (64, 160). The GEMM would be: C[64, 131084] = W[64, 160] @ X_col[160, 131084]. This is a small GEMM (64x160 @ 160x131084 ≈ 64x131084 output, M=64, K=160, N=131084). For cuBLAS, this is a "tall-and-skinny" GEMM from the perspective of M=64 being small. cuBLAS might handle this well, but the im2col step requires constructing X_col. For each column, we read 160 floats from scattered locations in input. This im2col can be done in a separate kernel. But then we have two kernels and extra memory for X_col (160 * 131084 * 4 ≈ 84 MB). Alternatively, we can fuse im2col and GEMM, which is what we're doing with the custom kernel. For a fused kernel, I think the best approach is: **Fused Kernel Strategy:** - Each thread computes one output element (or a small vector). - Coalesce memory access by having threads in a warp read consecutive input elements. - For the weight, since it's small (40KB), it stays in L2 cache nicely. But if I compute one output element per thread, each thread needs to loop over c_in (32) and k (5), doing 160 loads of input and 160 loads of weight. 320 loads total, each 4 bytes = 1280 bytes of register traffic. For the output to be written once (4 bytes), the arithmetic intensity is 160*2 / 1284 ≈ 0.25 FLOPs/byte, very bandwidth bound. With the weight in L2 cache, the main bandwidth is input. Each input element is read 5 times (for different k) by threads in the same warp? No, for different output positions in the warp. Actually, let me reconsider tiling. If I compute multiple output channels per input load, I can reuse input data. **Better approach: Input-stationary tiling** For a tile of output positions (e.g., 64 consecutive o's): - Load the input range needed for all these positions into shared memory. - For each c_out channel (or a group), load the corresponding weights. - All threads in the block compute their output elements using the shared input and weights. This reuses loaded input data across all output channels in the block. Tile size considerations: - Output positions per block: 128 (to get good occupancy with 128 or 256 threads) - Output channels per block: 4 or 8 - Input channels processed sequentially: all 32 For block configuration: - blockDim.x = 128 threads for output positions - blockDim.y = 4 threads for output channels - Total threads = 512 Actually, a simpler configuration: 256 threads total. Each thread computes one (c_out, o) pair. But o is the fast dimension. Wait, with Grid-Stride Loops or similar patterns, we can have each thread compute multiple output positions. Let me design a specific kernel: ```cuda template<int BLOCK_SIZE_O, int BLOCK_SIZE_C> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation ) { // Each block processes: // - One batch element // - BLOCK_SIZE_C output channels // - BLOCK_SIZE_O output positions int n = blockIdx.z; // batch int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_SIZE_C; int o_base = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_SIZE_O; int tid = threadIdx.x; // linear thread ID int thread_o = tid % BLOCK_SIZE_O; int thread_c = tid / BLOCK_SIZE_O; // Load input for this tile into shared memory // The input range needed is [o_base - (K-1)*d, o_base + BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1] // Let min_in = max(0, o_base - (K-1)*d) // Let max_in = min(in_length - 1, o_base + BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1) __shared__ float s_input[in_channels][BLOCK_SIZE_O + (kernel_size - 1) * dilation]; __shared__ float s_weight[in_channels][BLOCK_SIZE_C][kernel_size]; // ... load input and weight ... // ... compute ... } ``` The problem with this approach is that the shared memory size depends on runtime parameters, which is tricky for static allocation. We can use dynamic shared memory or template with maximum sizes. For the given problem: K=5, d=3, so max input overlap is 12. BLOCK_SIZE_O could be 64 or 128. Shared memory: - s_input: in_channels * (BLOCK_SIZE_O + 12) floats. For in_channels=32, BLOCK_SIZE_O=128: 32 * 140 * 4 = 17,920 bytes - s_weight: in_channels * BLOCK_SIZE_C * K floats. For BLOCK_SIZE_C=8: 32 * 8 * 5 * 4 = 5,120 bytes Total: ~23KB per block. With 228KB shared memory per SM, we can run multiple blocks concurrently. For 512 threads per block, that's maybe 1 block per SM or more depending on registers. Occupancy: With 512 threads per block, max blocks per SM = min(32, 2048/512) = 4 on H100? H100 has max 2048 threads per SM, so 4 blocks of 512 threads. But if shared memory is 23KB, max blocks = 228/23 ≈ 9. So occupancy is limited by threads: 4 blocks * 512 = 2048 threads = full occupancy. Wait, H100 has 32 blocks per SM max, 2048 threads per SM max. 512 threads per block → 4 blocks per SM max. So we'd get full thread occupancy with 4 blocks per SM. But we need enough blocks to saturate the GPU. Total blocks: - batch = 32 - out_channels = 64, BLOCK_SIZE_C = 8 → 8 channel blocks - out_length = 131084, BLOCK_SIZE_O = 128 → ~1024 position blocks Total blocks: 32 * 8 * 1024 = 262,144 blocks. With 132 SMs (H100 has ~132-144 SMs depending on SKU), we have plenty of blocks. This design should work well. But let me think about register pressure. Each thread does a loop over in_channels (32) and kernel_size (5), accumulating in a register. That's simple. Now, a potential issue: loading s_input. Since the required input range for each block is [o_base - 12, o_base + BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1] ∩ [0, in_length-1]. For the first block (o_base = 0): needed input is [0, BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1] (since o_base - 12 < 0). For o_base >= 12: needed input is [o_base - 12, o_base + BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1]. All threads in the block can participate in loading input. With BLOCK_SIZE_O=128 and in_channels=32, total input floats to load = 32 * 140 = 4480. With 512 threads, each thread loads ~9 floats. Similarly for weights: 32 * 8 * 5 = 1280 floats, each thread loads ~3 floats. For the compute, each thread computes for one (c_out, o) pair. Loop over c_in=32 and k=5. 160 MACs per thread. After loading shared memory, the MACs use fast shared memory accesses. Wait, but if each thread computes one output element, and threadIdx.x is linear from 0 to 511: - thread_o = tid % BLOCK_SIZE_O (0 to 127) - thread_c = tid / BLOCK_SIZE_O (0 to 3) This means for a fixed o, only BLOCK_SIZE_C threads (here 4) compute different c_out channels. And the BLOCK_SIZE_O threads for a fixed c_out compute consecutive o's. Is this good for shared memory access? For threads with the same thread_c (same c_out) but different thread_o, they access: s_input[c_in][thread_o + offset] for various offsets. These are consecutive in shared memory, so no bank conflicts. For threads with the same thread_o but different thread_c, they access different weights: s_weight[c_in][thread_c][k] These also should be fine depending on layout. Actually, let me reconsider the shared memory layout for weights. Option 1: s_weight[c_in][c_out_local][k] - This means for fixed c_in and k, c_out_local varies in the last dimension if we use float3 or similar. Actually, with float, s_weight[c_in][c_out_local][k] means: Address = ((c_in * BLOCK_SIZE_C + c_out_local) * K + k) For threads in a warp with same c_in, k, but different c_out_local: they access consecutive addresses → coalesced in shared memory. Option 2: s_weight[c_out_local][c_in][k] - For threads with different c_out_local, they access blocks separated by in_channels*K. I think option 1 (or a flattened version) is fine. For s_input, the layout should be: s_input[c_in][pos_local] Where pos_local ranges from 0 to BLOCK_SIZE_O + 12 - 1. Actually, for pos_local, I need to map it properly. If the input range starts at in_start (which could be o_base - 12 or 0), then: s_input[c_in][p] = input[n, c_in, in_start + p] For output position o = o_base + thread_o: The required inputs are at global indices o - d*k for k=0..4. For k=0: o_base + thread_o - 0. Local index = (o_base + thread_o) - (o_base - 12) = thread_o + 12. So the local indices used are thread_o, thread_o+3, thread_o+6, thread_o+9, thread_o+12. The accesses are naturally consecutive for threads in a warp, so there are no shared memory bank conflicts as long as the thread_o dimension is the fast-changing one. For thread_o=3: k=0 (3), k=1 (0), valid. So we need to handle boundary conditions. For simplicity, we can just set the shared memory to 0 for out-of-bounds regions and always do the full compute. Or we can add explicit bounds checks. Given that out_length = in_length + 12, the boundary regions are only at the beginning and end. I'll use a simpler approach without shared memory first, then see if I need it. **Simple Global Memory Kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel_simple( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation, int use_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_outputs = batch_size * out_channels * out_length; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; // Decode index int n = idx / (out_channels * out_length); int rem = idx % (out_channels * out_length); int c_out = rem / out_length; int o = rem % out_length; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < in_channels; c_in++) { for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; k++) { int in_pos = o - k * dilation; if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) { float in_val = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + in_pos]; float w_val = weight[((c_in * out_channels) + c_out) * kernel_size + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } if (use_bias) sum += bias[c_out]; output[((n * out_channels) + c_out) * out_length + o] = sum; } ``` This is straightforward but might be memory-bound because: 1. Input and weight are both read from global memory 2. Weight is read in order: weight[c_in][c_out][k]. For threads with different c_in, this is strided. For threads in a warp, if they have the same c_out but different c_in, they might have poor coalescing for weight loads. Wait, let me think about the memory access pattern for the simple kernel. If each thread computes one (n, c_out, o), and threads in a block have consecutive idx: - idx 0: (n=0, c_out=0, o=0) - idx 1: (n=0, c_out=0, o=1) - idx 2: (n=0, c_out=0, o=2) - ... - idx 131084: (n=0, c_out=1, o=0) So a warp of 32 threads computing consecutive idx values: - All have the same n (first several warps) - Most have the same c_out (131084 / 32 = 4096 warps per c_out) - o varies by thread: thread i has o = base_o + i For weight access: weight[((c_in * out_channels) + c_out) * kernel_size + k] For a fixed c_out and varying c_in: Thread i might have different c_in in the inner loop, but all threads in the warp iterate c_in from 0 to 31. Within the c_in loop, for fixed c_in and k: All threads in the warp access weight[((c_in * out_channels) + c_out) * kernel_size + k] with the same c_out but potentially different c_in... wait no, at a given c_in iteration, ALL threads in the warp have the same c_out but might have different c_in values? No! In the simple kernel encoding: idx increases first along o, then c_out, then n. So for a warp: - Same n and c_out (since out_length = 131084, and 131084 >> 32) - Different o values (consecutive) So for a warp: n and c_out are constant, o varies. Weight access: weight[((c_in * out_channels) + c_out) * kernel_size + k] For a fixed c_out, varying c_in, varying k: All threads in the warp access the SAME weight element at a given (c_in, k) combination! This is excellent for L1/L2 cache. The weight for a given c_out and all c_in can stay in L1 cache for the entire warp. Input access: input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + (o - k*dilation)] For fixed n, c_in, varying o: consecutive o means the input indices are consecutive (minus k*dilation which is constant for fixed k). So for k=0: input indices are o, o+1, o+2, ... → perfectly coalesced! For k=1: input indices are o-3, o-2, o-1, ... → perfectly coalesced! etc. This actually means the simple kernel has great memory access patterns! Let me reconsider the shared memory approach. In the simple kernel: 1. Warp shares n and c_out. 2. Threads in warp access consecutive input elements for each (c_in, k). 3. Threads in warp access the SAME weight elements for each (c_in, k). The weight access is broadcast from L1 cache. The input access is coalesced from global memory. So the simple kernel should actually have pretty good performance! The main limitation is that it reads weight from global memory (or L2/L1 cache) and input from global memory. But input is read K=5 times per output element (once for each k). Since the same input element is reused across different c_out values (in different warps), but not within a warp. L2 cache might help a bit. For a warp computing c_out=0, o=0..31: Input reads for c_in=0, k=0: input[0..31] (coalesced, 128 bytes) For k=1: input[-3..28] (some invalid, but input[0..28] is 116 bytes, coalesced) For c_in=1, k=0: input[in_length..in_length+31] = input[131072..131103] but wait, indexing is ((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + pos. For c_in=1, k=0: pos=0..31, so addresses are n*in_channels*in_length + 1*in_length + pos. These are consecutive: in_length apart from c_in=0 addresses. Wait, the input layout is (batch, channels, length), so it's channel-major in the last dimension. For PyTorch default, it's NCHW or NCL. The strides are: stride[0] = in_channels * in_length, stride[1] = in_length, stride[2] = 1. So input[n, c_in, pos] is at address: n * (in_channels * in_length) + c_in * in_length + pos. For consecutive c_in values with the same pos, the addresses are in_length apart = 131072 * 4 = 524288 bytes = 512 KB. Two consecutive c_in channels at the same pos are 512KB apart. That's much larger than L1 cache (maybe 128KB or so), so accessing c_in=0 then c_in=1 at the same pos will be L1 misses (but L2 hits if the data was recently accessed). For c_in=0, all pos accessed are loaded into L1. For c_in=1, all pos accessed are 512KB away. For a warp accessing pos=0..31 at c_in=0, that's 128 bytes. At c_in=1, pos=0..31, that's another 128 bytes 512KB away. L1 cache line is typically 128 bytes, so we get one cache line per 128 bytes for c_in=0, but the next access at c_in=1 is completely unrelated. So for the simple kernel, the inner loop structure is: For c_in in 0..31: For k in 0..4: load input[n, c_in, o-k*d] for all threads in warp (coalesced, 128 bytes) load weight[c_in, c_out, k] (same for all threads, cached) multiply-add Memory traffic per warp per (c_in, k): - 128 bytes of input (one cache line) - 4 bytes of weight (broadcasting) For 32 c_in and 5 k: 32 * 5 * 128 = 20,480 bytes input, 32*5*4 = 640 bytes weight. Output writes: 32 * 4 = 128 bytes. Total: ~21KB of memory traffic for 32 threads * 32 c_in * 5 k = 5120 MACs = 10240 FLOPs. 10240 / 21504 = 0.48 FLOPs/byte. This is bandwidth bound, but H100 has 3.35 TB/s bandwidth. At 0.48 FLOPs/byte, the theoretical max performance is 3.35 * 0.48 = 1.6 TFLOPs. Total work is 43 GFLOPs for the whole problem, so time = 43 / 1600 = 27ms. That's quite slow. But wait, this assumes everything goes to DRAM. Hmm, using shared memory for input could reduce global memory traffic IF we compute multiple c_out channels per block. For a block computing T_cout channels and T_o positions: - Load input once for the tile. - Compute all T_cout output channels using the same input tile. - This reuses input data T_cout times from fast shared memory instead of re-reading from L2/global. Range size: up to 44 positions (for o_base >= 12) or fewer for first block. For o_base >= 12: positions from o_base-12 to o_base+31, total 44 positions. Shared memory for input: in_channels * 44 floats = 32 * 44 * 4 = 5632 bytes. Shared memory for weights: in_channels * 4 * 5 floats = 32 * 4 * 5 * 4 = 2560 bytes. Total: ~8KB. Very small! With 8KB shared memory, we can run many blocks per SM. 228KB / 8KB = 28 blocks. But max threads limit is 2048, and 128 threads per block → 16 blocks per SM. So occupancy is limited by threads. Is 16 blocks per SM enough to hide latency? With 132 SMs, total concurrent blocks = 132 * 16 = 2112 blocks. But we need 32 * (64/4) * (131084/32) = 32 * 16 * 4097 = 2,096,384 blocks. So we have plenty. Actually, wait. 131084 / 32 = 4096.375, so we need 4097 or 4096 blocks with some padding. Let's say 4097. Total blocks = 32 * 16 * 4097 = 2,097,024. Time per block: Each block computes 128 outputs. Each output requires 32*5=160 MACs. Total MACs per block = 128 * 160 = 20,480. At peak 67 TFLOPS = 67,000 GFLOPS, total time for compute = 2M blocks * 20K FLOPs / 67T FLOPs/s = 0.6ms. But we're bandwidth bound. For memory bandwidth at 3.35 TB/s: Total traffic = input (read once per block, but reused across c_out tiles) + output (written once) + weights (read once per block). Wait, with the shared memory tiling, input is read once from global memory per (n, o_tile) and reused for all c_out tiles that process the same batch and o_tile. For 16 c_out tiles (64/4), the input is read once and reused 16 times! So total input reads from global memory: batch * num_o_tiles * in_channels * tile_input_size * 4 bytes. = 32 * 4097 * 32 * 44 * 4 ≈ 745MB. But without shared memory, input would be read 64 times (once per c_out), so total input traffic would be 32GB. With shared memory, it's ~745MB. Huge reduction! Weight traffic: 32 * 16 * 4097 * 32 * 4 * 5 * 4 ≈ 5.3GB? No wait, weights are 40KB total. Each block reads 32*4*5 = 640 floats = 2.56KB of weights. Total weight traffic = 2M blocks * 2.56KB = 5.3GB. But! Weights for different c_out tiles with the same (n, o_tile) but different c_out_base are different. However, weights are 40KB total, which fits in L2 cache! So in reality, weights are read from L2, not DRAM. The DRAM traffic for weights is just 40KB (read once into L2). Output traffic: 32 * 64 * 131084 * 4 ≈ 1.07GB. This must be written to DRAM. So total DRAM traffic: 745MB input + 1.07GB output ≈ 1.82GB. At 3.35 TB/s: 1.82 / 3350 = 0.54ms. This ignores some overhead but suggests the shared memory approach could be very fast. The question is: can we achieve near-peak bandwidth? If so, 0.54ms is achievable. But what about L2 cache? For input data to stay in L2 between c_out tiles, the blocks processing the same (n, o_tile) but different c_out must be scheduled closely together. The GPU scheduler might not guarantee this. However, with 16 c_out tiles per (n, o_tile), and 4097 o_tiles per n, there are 65,552 blocks per n. If these are all enqueued, the scheduler might execute them in some order. But there's no guarantee that c_out=0 and c_out=1 for the same o_tile run back-to-back. To maximize L2 reuse, we should structure the grid so that c_out is the fastest-changing dimension... actually cuda grids process in some order but it's not guaranteed. However, if we launch all blocks and the scheduler uses a FIFO or spatial ordering, blocks with close blockIdx values might run close together. If I structure the grid as: gridDim.x = num_o_tiles gridDim.y = num_cout_tiles gridDim.z = batch Then consecutive blockIdx.x increments change o_tile. For a fixed o_tile and batch, c_out tiles are consecutive in blockIdx.y. Actually, blocks are launched and scheduled somewhat arbitrarily. But cuDNN/cublas often uses similar tilings and achieves good performance through cache. Alternatively, if I put c_out as the fastest-changing dimension in a larger block: A block processes T_cout=64 (all output channels) and T_o=32. Then input is reused across all 64 c_out channels within the same block. Block size: 64 * 32 = 2048 threads. But max threads per block is 1024 on most GPUs, and 2048 might not be supported. Actually, H100 supports 1024 threads per block max. So 2048 is too many. What about T_cout=32, T_o=32? Block = 1024 threads. Input needed: 32 * 44 floats = 5632 bytes. Weights: 32 * 32 * 5 = 5120 floats = 20,480 bytes. Total shared: ~26KB. With 1024 threads per block and 26KB shared memory, max blocks per SM = min(2048/1024, 228/26) = min(2, 8) = 2. Occupancy: 2 blocks * 1024 threads = 2048 threads = full! But only 2 blocks per SM. That's fine if we have enough blocks total: 32 * (64/32) * (131084/32) = 32 * 2 * 4097 = 262,208 blocks. With 132 SMs and 2 blocks each = 264 concurrent blocks. So it takes 262208 / 264 = 993 waves. This should still be fine for throughput. Wait, but 1024 threads per block with 2 blocks per SM means total warps = 64 warps per SM. The GPU schedules warps, not blocks, so 64 warps per SM should be enough to hide latency. But 1024 threads per block might have high register usage. If each thread uses, say, 32 registers, total = 32K registers. Per SM limit is 64K, so max 2 blocks anyway. If each thread uses 64 registers, total = 64K, exactly one block per SM. Register pressure could limit occupancy. I think 256 threads per block is a safer choice. Let me try: - T_cout = 8, T_o = 32. - Block: 256 threads (arranged as 32 x 8, with x=position, y=channel). - Input needed: 32 * 44 floats = 5.6KB. - Weights: 32 * 8 * 5 = 1.28K floats = 5.1KB. - Total shared: ~11KB. With 256 threads and 11KB shared memory: Max blocks per SM = min(2048/256, 228/11) = min(8, 20) = 8. Occupancy = 8 * 256 = 2048 threads. Full occupancy! This is great. And total blocks = 32 * (64/8) * (131084/32) = 32 * 8 * 4097 = 1,048,832 blocks. Plenty of parallelism. Let me design the kernel with this configuration. **Kernel with T_cout=8, T_o=32:** ```cuda template<int BLOCK_SIZE_O=32, int BLOCK_SIZE_C=8> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation ) { // Block indices int n = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_SIZE_C; int o_block = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_SIZE_O; // Thread indices int o_local = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int c_local = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 // Global output channel int c_out = c_out_block + c_local; if (c_out >= out_channels) return; // Calculate input range needed int o_start = o_block; // first output position in this block int o_end = o_block + BLOCK_SIZE_O - 1; // last output position // Input range: [o_start - (K-1)*d, o_end] int in_start = o_start - (kernel_size - 1) * dilation; int in_end = o_end; // Clamp to valid input range int in_start_clamped = max(in_start, 0); int in_end_clamped = min(in_end, in_length - 1); int in_tile_size = in_end_clamped - in_start_clamped + 1; // Shared memory extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; // [in_channels][in_tile_size_padded] float* s_weight = s_mem + in_channels * in_tile_size; // [in_channels][BLOCK_SIZE_C][kernel_size] // Load input into shared memory // Need to load in_channels * in_tile_size floats // Use all threads cooperatively int total_input_elements = in_channels * in_tile_size; for (int idx = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; idx < total_input_elements; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int c_in = idx / in_tile_size; int pos = idx % in_tile_size; int global_pos = in_start_clamped + pos; s_input[c_in * in_tile_size + pos] = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + global_pos]; } // Load weights into shared memory // weight shape: (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size) int total_weight_elements = in_channels * BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size; for (int idx = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; idx < total_weight_elements; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { // Decode: c_in * BLOCK_SIZE_C * K + c_local * K + k int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % kernel_size; tmp /= kernel_size; int c_l = tmp % BLOCK_SIZE_C; tmp /= BLOCK_SIZE_C; int c_in = tmp; int global_c_out = c_out_block + c_l; if (global_c_out < out_channels) { s_weight[c_in * (BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size) + c_l * kernel_size + k] = weight[((c_in * out_channels) + global_c_out) * kernel_size + k]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute output float sum = 0.0f; int o = o_block + o_local; if (o < out_length) { for (int c_in = 0; c_in < in_channels; c_in++) { for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; k++) { int in_pos = o - k * dilation; if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) { int s_in_idx = in_pos - in_start_clamped; float in_val = s_input[c_in * in_tile_size + s_in_idx]; float w_val = s_weight[c_in * (BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size) + c_local * kernel_size + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } } if (o < out_length) { output[((n * out_channels) + c_out) * out_length + o] = sum; } } ``` Wait, there might be shared memory bank conflicts. With K=5, consecutive threads access addresses 5 apart. Since shared memory has 32 banks (usually), and 5 is coprime to 32, there should be no bank conflicts for 8 c_local values. For threads with the same c_local but different c_in: addresses differ by BLOCK_SIZE_C * K = 40. 40 and 32 have gcd 8, so threads with c_in differing by 8 would have bank conflicts. But with blockDim.y=8, c_local ranges from 0 to 7, and threads in a warp have the same c_local if arranged by (c_local, o_local)... Warp 1: threads 32-63: threadIdx.y=1, threadIdx.x=0..31. c_local=1 for the entire warp! This means each warp computes a different c_local! int c_out_block = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_SIZE_C; int o_block = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_SIZE_O; int o_local = threadIdx.x; int c_local = threadIdx.y; int c_out = c_out_block + c_local; if (c_out >= out_channels) return; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_weight = s_mem + in_channels * in_tile_size; int total_input = in_channels * in_tile_size; int num_threads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_input; idx += num_threads) { int c_in = idx / in_tile_size; int pos = idx % in_tile_size; int global_pos = in_start_clamped + pos; s_input[c_in * in_tile_size + pos] = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + global_pos]; } int total_weight = in_channels * BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_weight; idx += num_threads) { int c_in = idx / (BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size); int rem = idx % (BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size); int c_l = rem / kernel_size; int k = rem % kernel_size; int global_c_out = c_out_block + c_l; int in_tile_max = BLOCK_SIZE_O + (kernel_size - 1) * dilation; size_t shared_mem_size = (in_channels * in_tile_max + in_channels * BLOCK_SIZE_C * kernel_size) * sizeof(float); conv_transpose1d_kernel<BLOCK_SIZE_O, BLOCK_SIZE_C><<<grid, block, shared_mem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), use_bias ? bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, in_channels, out_channels, in_length, out_length, kernel_size, dilation, use_bias ); return output; } ''' cpp_source = r''' torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int use_bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation ); ''' ``` Wait, I need to be careful with the string formatting. The compiler might be able to optimize the branch if it can determine all threads in the warp take the same path. k * dilation;\n if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) {\n sum += in_ptr[in_pos] * w_ptr[k];\n }\n }\n }\n \n if (use_bias) {\n sum += bias[c_out];\n }\n \n output[((n * out_channels) + c_out) * out_length + o] = (float)sum;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n int use_bias,\n int stride, int padding, int dilation\n) {\n int batch_size = input.size(0);\n int in_channels = input.size(1);\n int in_length = input.size(2);\n int out_channels = weight.size(1);\n int kernel_size = weight.size(2);\n \n int out_length = (in_length - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1;\n \n auto output = torch::empty({batch_size, out_channels, out_length}, input.options());\n \n const int BO = 16;\n const int BC = 8;\n \n dim3 block(BO, BC);\n dim3 grid(\n (out_length + BO - Let me think about this more carefully by considering what transposed convolution actually does. Transposed convolution reverses the "forward" and "backward" directions. If standard conv computes `y = A @ x` (for some sparse matrix A representing the convolution), then transposed conv computes `x = A^T @ y`. For standard 1D conv with stride=1, padding=0, dilation=d: - Matrix A has dimensions (out_length, in_length) for each channel pair. - A[o_output, o_input] = w[k] where o_output = o_input + k*d (i.e., A[i, i+k*d] = w[k]) So A^T has A^T[o_input, o_output] = w[k] where o_input = o_output + k*d. Or more precisely, A^T[i, j] = A[j, i] = w[k] where j = i + k*d, i.e., i = j - k*d. So for conv transpose: output[o] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k} input[o - k*d] * w[k] where the sum is over valid k. BUT here's the CATCH: for standard conv, the output length is different from input length when padding and dilation are involved. Step 1: `w_perm = w.permute(1, 0, 2)` gives `(out_channels, in_channels, K)` Step 2: `w_flipped = w_perm.flip(2)` gives `(out_channels, in_channels, K)` with kernel flipped Then `Conv1d(x, w_flipped, stride=1, padding=(K-1)*d, dilation=d)` gives the same result! Let me draft the kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cublas_v2.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> __global__ void im2col_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ A, int batch_size, int in_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation, int patch_size ) { int n = blockIdx.z; int o = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= batch_size || o >= out_length || idx >= patch_size) return; int c_in = idx / kernel_size; int k = idx % kernel_size; int in_pos = o - k * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) { val = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + in_pos]; } A[((n * out_length) + o) * patch_size + idx] = val; } __global__ void add_bias_kernel( float* output, const float* bias, int batch_size, int out_channels, int out_length ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * out_channels * out_length; if (idx >= total) return; int n = idx / (out_channels * out_length); int rem = idx % (out_channels * out_length); int c_out = rem / out_length; int o = rem % out_length; output[idx] += bias[c_out]; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int use_bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { int batch_size = input.size(0); int in_channels = input.size(1); int in_length = input.size(2); int out_channels = weight.size(1); int kernel_size = weight.size(2); int out_length = (in_length - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1; int patch_size = in_channels * kernel_size; auto A = torch::empty({batch_size, out_length, patch_size}, input.options()); auto output = torch::empty({batch_size, out_length, out_channels}, input.options()); // im2col dim3 block_im2col(16, 16); dim3 grid_im2col( (patch_size + 15) / 16, (out_length + 15) / 16, batch_size ); im2col_kernel<<<grid_im2col, block_im2col>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), A.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, in_channels, in_length, out_length, kernel_size, dilation, patch_size ); // cublas GEMM cublasHandle_t handle = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle(); // Reshape weight to [patch_size, out_channels] row-major // In Python, weight.permute(2, 0, 1).contiguous().view(patch_size, out_channels) // For now, assume weight is already in the right format. float alpha = 1.0f, beta = 0.0f; // C = A * B where A is [batch, m, k], B is [k, n] // Using strided batched: C[b] = A[b] * B // A[b] is [out_length, patch_size] row-major -> [m, k] // B is [patch_size, out_channels] row-major -> [k, n] // C[b] is [out_length, out_channels] row-major -> [m, n] // cublas is column-major. We want C_row = A_row * B_row // C_col = C_row^T = B_row^T * A_row^T = B_col * A_col // Where B_col = [n, k], A_col = [k, m] // So we call: cublasSgemmStridedBatched(..., n, m, k, ...) // with A_ptr = B_col, B_ptr = A_col cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, out_channels, out_length, patch_size, &alpha, weight.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, 0, A.data_ptr<float>(), patch_size, out_length * patch_size, &beta, output.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, out_length * out_channels, batch_size ); if (use_bias) { int total = batch_size * out_length * out_channels; int threads = 256; add_bias_kernel<<<(total + threads - 1) / threads, threads>>>( output.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, out_channels, out_length ); } return output.reshape({batch_size, out_channels, out_length}); } ``` Wait, for the weight, I need to make sure it's in the right format. Now for cublas, B_row is [patch_size, out_channels] = [k, n] in my earlier notation, stored row-major. For the cublas call: C = B_col * A_col, where: - B_col = [n, k] column-major = B_row^T - Since B_row is row-major [k, n], B_col[i, j] = B_row[j, i], and B_col is stored with ldb = n (distance between columns). A_col[i, j] = A_row[j, i]. A_row rows are contiguous with length k, so A_col is column-major with lda = k. - C_col = [n, m] column-major = C_row^T - C_row is [m, n] row-major. C_col[i, j] = C_row[j, i]. If C_row rows are contiguous with length n, C_col is column-major with ldc = n. So the cublas call: ```cpp cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, n, m, k, // n=out_channels, m=out_length, k=patch_size &alpha, weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), n, 0, // B_col: [n, k], ldb=n, strideB=0 A.data_ptr<float>(), k, m * k, // A_col: [k, m], lda=k, strideA=m*k &beta, output.data_ptr<float>(), n, m * n, // C_col: [n, m], ldc=n, strideC=m*n batch_size ); ``` Yes, this is correct! Now for the cublas call in the CUDA extension: A = im2col tensor, shape [batch, out_length, patch_size], row-major B = weight_flat, shape [patch_size, out_channels], row-major C = output, shape [batch, out_length, out_channels], row-major We want C[b] = A[b] * B. For cublas column-major, C_col = B_col * A_col where: - B_col = B^T = [out_channels, patch_size] - A_col = A^T = [patch_size, out_length] Wait, earlier I said C = B_col * A_col where C is [n, m]. C_col[j, i] = C_row[i, j]. C_row[i, j] = sum_k A_row[i, k] * B_row[k, j] C_col[j, i] = sum_k A_row[i, k] * B_row[k, j] B_col[j, k] = B_row[k, j] A_col[k, i] = A_row[i, k] So C_col[j, i] = sum_k B_col[j, k] * A_col[k, i] Which means C_col = B_col * A_col. In cublas: C of size [n, m] = op(B) * op(A) where op(B) is n×k and op(A) is k×m. B_col is [n, k] column-major. If op(B) = B_col (no transpose), then B_ptr points to B_col with ldb = n. A_col is [k, m] column-major. If op(A) = A_col (no transpose), then A_ptr points to A_col with lda = k. C_col is [n, m] column-major. C_ptr with ldc = n. So the call is exactly what I wrote before: ```cpp cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, out_channels, out_length, patch_size, &alpha, B.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, 0, A.data_ptr<float>(), patch_size, out_length * patch_size, &beta, C.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, out_length * out_channels, batch_size ); ``` Wait, I need to double-check the argument order. In cublasSgemmStridedBatched: ```cpp cublasStatus_t cublasSgemmStridedBatched(cublasHandle_t handle, cublasOperation_t transa, cublasOperation_t transb, int m, int n, int k, const float *alpha, const float *A, int lda, long long int strideA, const float *B, int ldb, long long int strideB, const float *beta, float *C, int ldc, long long int strideC, int batchCount) ``` Wait! The first matrix is A, second is B. The formula is C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C. So op(A) is m×k, op(B) is k×n, C is m×n. In my case, I want C_col = B_col * A_col. So op(A) = B_col (which is n×k), op(B) = A_col (which is k×m). But cublas requires op(A) to be m×k and op(B) to be k×n. Here C_col is n×m. So: m = n (output channels = 64) n = m (output length = 131084) k = patch_size (160) op(A) = B_col = [n, k] = [64, 160], so transa = CUBLAS_OP_N. op(B) = A_col = [k, m] = [160, 131084], so transb = CUBLAS_OP_N. A_ptr = B_col data = weight_flat data lda = 64 (number of rows in B_col) strideA = 0 (same weight for all batches) B_ptr = A_col data = im2col data ldb = 160 (number of rows in A_col) strideB = out_length * patch_size = 131084 * 160 C_ptr = C_col data = output data ldc = 64 (number of rows in C_col) strideC = out_length * out_channels = 131084 * 64 batchCount = batch_size = 32 So: ```cpp cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, out_channels, out_length, patch_size, &alpha, weight_flat.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, 0, A.data_ptr<float>(), patch_size, out_length * patch_size, &beta, output.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, out_length * out_channels, batch_size ); ``` This computes C_col (64 × 131084) = B_col (64 × 160) * A_col (160 × 131084), which is correct. And C_col[i, j] corresponds to output_row[j, i], which is [batch, out_length, out_channels]. Wait, but cublas Sgemm memory order for C is column-major. If C_ptr points to our output tensor which is [batch, out_length, out_channels] row-major, then: output_row[b, o, c] should equal C_col[c, o]. In row-major: output[b, o, c] is at offset: b*out_length*out_channels + o*out_channels + c In column-major terms for slice [c, o]: C_col[c, o] is at offset: c + o*ldc where ldc = 64 (number of rows). offset = c + o*64 But our output at [o, c] has offset o*out_channels + c = o*64 + c. These are the same! So if ldc = 64 (out_channels), then the [batch, out_length, out_channels] row-major tensor IS column-major when viewed as [64, out_length] per batch! Wait, that's a profound insight! For a tensor of shape [out_length, out_channels] in row-major: - Viewed as a matrix with out_length rows and out_channels columns, it's row-major. - But if we view the SAME memory as a matrix with out_channels rows and out_length columns in column-major: Element at [c, o] in column-major: offset = c + o*out_channels. Element at [o, c] in row-major: offset = o*out_channels + c. These ARE the same! So [out_length, out_channels] row-major is exactly [out_channels, out_length] column-major! No permutation needed! This means: - Our output [batch, out_length, out_channels] row-major = [batch, 64, 131084] column-major per batch. - Our A [batch, out_length, patch_size] row-major = [batch, 160, 131084] column-major per batch. - Our B [patch_size, out_channels] row-major = [160, 64] column-major. These are the same! So [160, 64] row-major = [64, 160] column-major. A is [131084, 160] row-major. As column-major [160, 131084]: element [i, o] has offset i + o*160. Element [i, o] in column-major at offset i + o*k. These are indeed the same! So ANY row-major [m, k] tensor is identical to column-major [k, m] tensor in memory layout! This means: - A: [out_length, patch_size] row-major = [patch_size, out_length] column-major. lda = patch_size (since column-major [patch_size, out_length], lda = number of rows = patch_size). - B: [patch_size, out_channels] row-major = [out_channels, patch_size] column-major. ldb = out_channels. - C: [out_length, out_channels] row-major = [out_channels, out_length] column-major. ldc = out_channels. Great! So the cublas call is: ```cpp cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, // no transpose on either out_channels, out_length, patch_size, // m, n, k &alpha, B_ptr, out_channels, 0, // B: [m_B, k] col-major = [out_channels, patch_size], ldb = m_B = out_channels A_ptr, patch_size, out_length * patch_size, // A: [m_A, n] col-major = [patch_size, out_length], lda = m_A = patch_size &beta, C_ptr, out_channels, out_length * out_channels, // C: [m_C, n] col-major = [out_channels, out_length], ldc = m_C = out_channels batch_size ); ``` Wait, I need to be very careful about the dimensions: cublasSgemmStridedBatched computes: C_i = alpha * op(A_i) * op(B_i) + beta * C_i where op(A_i) is m×k, op(B_i) is k×n, C_i is m×n. Now for the im2col kernel for a chunk: ```cpp __global__ void im2col_chunk_kernel( const float* input, float* A, int o_start, int chunk_len, int batch_size, int in_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation, int patch_size ) { int n = blockIdx.z; int o_local = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= batch_size || o_local >= chunk_len || idx >= patch_size) return; int o = o_start + o_local; int c_in = idx / kernel_size; int k = idx % kernel_size; int in_pos = o - k * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) { val = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + in_pos]; } A[((n * chunk_len) + o_local) * patch_size + idx] = val; } ``` This should work. It's a bit of overhead but for a single call it's fine. ```cpp cublasHandle_t handle; cublasCreate(&handle); // ... gemm ... cublasDestroy(&handle); ``` But creating/destroying a handle per forward pass is bad for performance. Let me use a cached handle. ```cpp static cublasHandle_t get_cublas_handle() { static cublasHandle_t handle = nullptr; if (handle == nullptr) { cublasCreate(&handle); } return handle; } ``` This is thread-safe in the sense that it's initialized once. But it's not perfectly thread-safe in initialization. For simplicity in this task, it should be fine. Actually, a static local variable in C++11 is thread-safe for initialization. So: ```cpp cublasHandle_t getHandle() { static cublasHandle_t handle = [](){ cublasHandle_t h; cublasCreate(&h); return h; }(); return handle; } ``` This is safe and simple. Now, do I need to link `-lcublas`? In PyTorch, cublas is usually linked. Let me add it to be safe. On most PyTorch installations, they should be in the CUDA toolkit path. Let me write the full Python module: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cublas_v2.h> cublasHandle_t getCublasHandle() { static cublasHandle_t handle = [](){ cublasHandle_t h; cublasCreate(&h); return h; }(); return handle; } __global__ void im2col_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ A, int batch_size, int in_channels, int in_length, int out_length, int kernel_size, int dilation, int patch_size ) { int n = blockIdx.z; int o = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= batch_size || o >= out_length || idx >= patch_size) return; int c_in = idx / kernel_size; int k = idx % kernel_size; int in_pos = o - k * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (in_pos >= 0 && in_pos < in_length) { val = input[((n * in_channels) + c_in) * in_length + in_pos]; } A[((n * out_length) + o) * patch_size + idx] = val; } __global__ void add_bias_kernel( float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ bias, int batch_size, int out_channels, int out_length ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * out_channels * out_length; if (idx >= total) return; int o = idx % out_length; int rem = idx / out_length; int c_out = rem % out_channels; output[idx] += bias[c_out]; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int use_bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { int batch_size = input.size(0); int in_channels = input.size(1); int in_length = input.size(2); int patch_size = weight.size(0); int out_channels = weight.size(1); int kernel_size = patch_size / in_channels; int out_length = (in_length - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1; auto A = torch::empty({batch_size, out_length, patch_size}, input.options()); auto output = torch::empty({batch_size, out_channels, out_length}, input.options()); // im2col dim3 block_im2col(16, 16); dim3 grid_im2col( (patch_size + 15) / 16, (out_length + 15) / 16, batch_size ); im2col_kernel<<<grid_im2col, block_im2col>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), A.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, in_channels, in_length, out_length, kernel_size, dilation, patch_size ); // cublas GEMM cublasHandle_t handle = getCublasHandle(); float alpha = 1.0f; float beta = 0.0f; // A is [batch, out_length, patch_size] row-major -> [batch, patch_size, out_length] col-major per batch // weight is [patch_size, out_channels] row-major -> [out_channels, patch_size] col-major // output is [batch, out_channels, out_length] row-major -> [batch, out_length, out_channels] col-major per batch cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_T, out_length, out_channels, patch_size, &alpha, A.data_ptr<float>(), patch_size, out_length * patch_size, weight.data_ptr<float>(), out_channels, 0, &beta, output.data_ptr<float>(), out_length, out_channels * out_length, batch_size ); if (use_bias) { int total = batch_size * out_channels * out_length; int threads = 256; add_bias_kernel<<<(total + threads - 1) / threads, threads>>>( output.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, out_channels, out_length ); } return output; } ''' cpp_source = r''' torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int use_bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation ); ''' conv_transpose1d_module = load_inline( name="conv_transpose1d_cublas", cpp_sources=[cpp_source], cuda_sources=[cuda_source], functions=["conv_transpose1d_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], extra_ldflags=["-lcublas"], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.stride = stride self.padding = padding self.dilation = dilation self.use_bias = bias self.conv_ref = nn.ConvTranspose1d( in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias ) def forward(self, x): x = x.contiguous() weight = self.conv_ref.weight # [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size] # Reshape weight to [in_channels*kernel_size, out_channels] with correct layout weight_t = weight.permute(0, 2, 1).contiguous().view(-1, weight.size(1)) if self.use_bias and self.conv_ref.bias is not None: bias_tensor = self.conv_ref.bias.contiguous() use_bias = 1 else: bias_tensor = torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) use_bias = 0 return conv_transpose1d_module.conv_transpose1d_cuda( x, weight_t, bias_tensor, use_bias, self.stride, self.padding, self.dilation ) ``` Let me compile and test this! Actually, wait. I want to double-check one thing: the A tensor is [batch, out_length, patch_size] row-major. When I pass it to cublas with CUBLAS_OP_T, A becomes [patch_size, out_length] col-major. In cublas column-major terms, the lda is the leading dimension of the ORIGINAL matrix (before transpose). The original A (before transpose) is [patch_size, out_length] col-major. lda = patch_size. Correct. For weight: [patch_size, out_channels] row-major. Before transpose, it's [out_channels, patch_size] col-major. ldb = out_channels. Correct. For output: [batch, out_channels, out_length] row-major. Before transpose, each batch is [out_length, out_channels] col-major. ldc = out_length. Correct. Wait, actually cublas computes C = op(A) * op(B). With transa = CUBLAS_OP_T, transb = CUBLAS_OP_T: op(A_i) = A_i^T. op(B_i) = B_i^T. A_i is [patch_size, out_length] col-major. A_i^T = [out_length, patch_size] col-major. B_i is [out_channels, patch_size] col-major. B_i^T = [patch_size, out_channels] col-major. C = [out_length, patch_size] * [patch_size, out_channels] = [out_length, out_channels] col-major. And [out_length, out_channels] col-major is the memory layout of [out_channels, out_length] row-major, which is our output! So output[b, c_out, o] corresponds to C_col[o, c_out] for that batch. Yes, this matches! One more check: cublasSgemmStridedBatched signature: ```cpp cublasStatus_t cublasSgemmStridedBatched( cublasHandle_t handle, cublasOperation_t transa, cublasOperation_t transb, int m, int n, int k, const float *alpha, const float *A, int lda, long long int strideA, const float *B, int ldb, long long int strideB, const float *beta, float *C, int ldc, long long int strideC, int batchCount) ``` Parameters: - m: number of rows of op(A) and C. op(A) = A_i^T = [out_length, patch_size]. m = out_length. - n: number of columns of op(B) and C. op(B) = B_i^T = [patch_size, out_channels]. n = out_channels. - k: number of columns of op(A) and rows of op(B). k = patch_size. - A: pointer to first A matrix. lda = leading dimension of A (before transpose). A is [patch_size, out_length] col-major. lda = patch_size. - strideA: stride between batch A matrices. A is [batch, out_length, patch_size] row-major. Per batch, A_i is [out_length, patch_size] row-major = [patch_size, out_length] col-major. Stride between batches = out_length * patch_size. - B: pointer to B matrix. ldb = leading dimension of B. B is [out_channels, patch_size] col-major (since [patch_size, out_channels] row-major). ldb = out_channels. - strideB: stride between batch B matrices. B is shared across batches. strideB = 0. - C: pointer to first C matrix. ldc = leading dimension of C. C is [out_length, out_channels] col-major per batch. ldc = out_length. - strideC: stride between batch C matrices. C is [batch, out_channels, out_length] row-major = [batch, out_length, out_channels] col-major per batch. Stride = out_length * out_channels. - batchCount: batch_size. This all checks out! Let me compile it. One more thing: `add_bias_kernel` output indexing. output is [batch, out_channels, out_length] row-major. offset = ((n * out_channels) + c_out) * out_length + o. In my add_bias_kernel: ```cpp int o = idx % out_length; int rem = idx / out_length; int c_out = rem % out_channels; ``` offset formula: idx maps to output[idx] since it's contiguous row-major with the last dimension out_length. Wait, with [batch, out_channels, out_length]: Total elements = batch * out_channels * out_length. If we iterate idx from 0 to total-1: idx = n * out_channels * out_length + c_out * out_length + o. So: o = idx % out_length c_out = (idx / out_length) % out_channels n = idx / (out_channels * out_length) My kernel computes: ```cpp int o = idx % out_length; int rem = idx / out_length; int c_out = rem % out_channels; ``` This correctly gets o and c_out (but doesn't need n for bias). offset = idx is correct because row-major and contiguous.
